:Example 6 Let n be a positive integer, and the sequence A: a_{1}, a_{2}, cdots, a_{n} be a sequence composed of 0 or 1, that is,a_{k}=0 or 1(1 leqslant k leqslant n).Find the number of sequences such that sum_{i=1}^{n} a_{i} leaves a remainder of 3 when divided by 4.

:[<think>Okay, so I need to find the number of binary sequences of length n where the sum of the elements is congruent to 3 modulo 4. Each element in the sequence is either 0 or 1. Let me think about how to approach this.First, the total number of possible sequences is 2^n since each position can be either 0 or 1. But I need to count only those sequences where the sum modulo 4 is 3. Hmm. This seems related to combinatorics and modular arithmetic. Maybe generating functions can help here?Let me recall that generating functions can be used to model the number of ways to achieve a certain sum. For each element in the sequence, the generating function would be (1 + x), since each term can contribute either 0 or 1. So, for n elements, the generating function is (1 + x)^n. The coefficient of x^k in this expansion gives the number of sequences with exactly k ones. But I need the sum of the coefficients where k 3 mod 4.To extract coefficients congruent to a certain value modulo 4, I can use roots of unity. There's a standard technique where you evaluate the generating function at the 4th roots of unity and combine the results appropriately. Let me remember the formula for such cases.The number of sequences where the sum is congruent to r modulo m is given by (1/m) * _{j=0}^{m-1} ^{-jr} * (1 + ^j)^n, where is a primitive mth root of unity. In this case, m=4 and r=3. So, substituting these values:Number of sequences = (1/4) [ (1 + 1)^n * ^{0} + (1 + )^n * ^{-3} + (1 + ^2)^n * ^{-6} + (1 + ^3)^n * ^{-9} ]But is a primitive 4th root of unity, so ^4 = 1, which allows us to simplify the exponents modulo 4. Let's compute each term step by step.First, ^0 = 1, ^1 = i, ^2 = -1, ^3 = -i.Let's substitute these into the formula:Term 1: (1 + 1)^n * ^{0} = 2^n * 1 = 2^nTerm 2: (1 + )^n * ^{-3} = (1 + i)^n * ^{-3}Since ^1 = i, ^{-3} is the same as ^{1} because -3 1 mod 4 (since -3 + 4 = 1). So ^{-3} = i.Thus, Term 2 becomes (1 + i)^n * iTerm 3: (1 + ^2)^n * ^{-6}^2 = -1, so 1 + ^2 = 0. Wait, 1 + (-1) = 0. So this term is 0^n * ^{-6}. But 0^n is 0 for n 1, which it is. So Term 3 is 0.Term 4: (1 + ^3)^n * ^{-9}^3 = -i, so 1 + ^3 = 1 - i^{-9} can be simplified by noting that ^4 = 1, so ^{-9} = ^{-9 mod 4} = ^{-1} = ^{3} = -i. Because -9 divided by 4 is -3 with remainder 3. So ^{-9} = ^3 = -i.Thus, Term 4 is (1 - i)^n * (-i)Putting all together:Number of sequences = (1/4) [ 2^n + (1 + i)^n * i + 0 + (1 - i)^n * (-i) ]Simplify terms 2 and 4:Let me compute (1 + i)^n * i - (1 - i)^n * i. Wait, no:Term 2 is (1 + i)^n * iTerm 4 is (1 - i)^n * (-i)So the sum of terms 2 and 4 is:(1 + i)^n * i + (1 - i)^n * (-i) = i*(1 + i)^n - i*(1 - i)^n = i [ (1 + i)^n - (1 - i)^n ]Therefore, the entire expression becomes:(1/4) [ 2^n + i*( (1 + i)^n - (1 - i)^n ) ]Hmm, okay. So now, perhaps we can express (1 + i)^n and (1 - i)^n in terms of polar form?Since 1 + i is 2 * e^{i/4} and 1 - i is 2 * e^{-i/4}. Therefore:(1 + i)^n = (2)^n * e^{i n /4}Similarly, (1 - i)^n = (2)^n * e^{-i n /4}So, substituting back into the expression:i * [ (2)^n e^{i n /4} - (2)^n e^{-i n /4} ] = i * (2)^n [ e^{i n /4} - e^{-i n /4} ] = i * (2)^n * 2i sin(n /4 )Because e^{i} - e^{-i} = 2i sin. So:i * 2i sin(n /4 ) * (2)^n = 2i^2 sin(n /4 ) * (2)^n = 2*(-1) sin(n /4 ) * (2)^n = -2 sin(n /4 ) * (2)^{n/2}Therefore, the entire expression for the number of sequences is:(1/4) [ 2^n - 2 * (2)^{n/2} sin(n /4 ) ]Wait, let me check:The term from above was:(1/4) [ 2^n + (i*( (1 + i)^n - (1 - i)^n )) ]Which after substitution becomes:(1/4) [ 2^n - 2 * (2)^{n/2} sin(n /4 ) ]But (2)^n = (2)^{n/2}, right. So yes. Therefore:Number of sequences = (1/4) [ 2^n - 2^{(n/2) + 1} sin(n /4 ) ]Hmm, that seems a bit complicated. Maybe there's a different approach?Alternatively, perhaps using recursion. Let me consider the possible remainders modulo 4. Lets denote by f(n, r) the number of sequences of length n where the sum is congruent to r mod 4.We need f(n, 3). Let's try to find a recurrence relation.For a sequence of length n, the last element can be 0 or 1. If it's 0, then the sum modulo 4 is the same as the sum of the first n-1 elements. If it's 1, then the sum is (sum of first n-1 elements +1) mod 4. Therefore, the recurrence relations are:f(n, 0) = f(n-1, 0) + f(n-1, 3)f(n, 1) = f(n-1, 1) + f(n-1, 0)f(n, 2) = f(n-1, 2) + f(n-1, 1)f(n, 3) = f(n-1, 3) + f(n-1, 2)With the base case n=0 (empty sequence), which has sum 0, so f(0,0)=1 and f(0,1)=f(0,2)=f(0,3)=0.This gives a system of linear recursions. Maybe we can solve this using linear algebra or generating functions.Alternatively, note that all four counts f(n,0), f(n,1), f(n,2), f(n,3) satisfy the same recurrence, each depending on the previous values. Let's write the recurrence in matrix form.Lets define the state vector as [f(n,0), f(n,1), f(n,2), f(n,3)]. Then, the next state vector is obtained by multiplying the current state vector by the transition matrix:[1 0 0 11 1 0 00 1 1 00 0 1 1]Wait, actually, let's check:For f(n,0) = f(n-1,0) + f(n-1,3)Similarly,f(n,1) = f(n-1,1) + f(n-1,0)f(n,2) = f(n-1,2) + f(n-1,1)f(n,3) = f(n-1,3) + f(n-1,2)So the transition matrix is:Row for f(n,0): [1, 0, 0, 1]Row for f(n,1): [1, 1, 0, 0]Row for f(n,2): [0, 1, 1, 0]Row for f(n,3): [0, 0, 1, 1]Yes, so the matrix is:[1 0 0 11 1 0 00 1 1 00 0 1 1]Hmm, this is a circulant matrix. Maybe diagonalizing it would help? But that might be complex. Alternatively, since the problem is symmetric, perhaps the solution can be expressed in terms of roots of unity.Alternatively, note that for each n, f(n,0) + f(n,1) + f(n,2) + f(n,3) = 2^n. Also, the generating function for each residue can be considered. Let's model this with generating functions.Let F_r(x) = _{n=0}^ f(n, r) x^nFrom the recurrence relations:F_0(x) = 1 + x F_0(x) + x F_3(x)F_1(x) = x F_1(x) + x F_0(x)F_2(x) = x F_2(x) + x F_1(x)F_3(x) = x F_3(x) + x F_2(x)Solving these equations:Starting with F_3:F_3(x) = x F_3(x) + x F_2(x)=> F_3(x)(1 - x) = x F_2(x)Similarly, F_2(x) = x F_2(x) + x F_1(x)=> F_2(x)(1 - x) = x F_1(x)F_1(x) = x F_1(x) + x F_0(x)=> F_1(x)(1 - x) = x F_0(x)F_0(x) = 1 + x F_0(x) + x F_3(x)=> F_0(x)(1 - x) = 1 + x F_3(x)Now, let's express each F_r in terms of F_{r-1}:From F_1: F_1 = (x / (1 - x)) F_0From F_2: F_2 = (x / (1 - x)) F_1 = (x^2 / (1 - x)^2) F_0From F_3: F_3 = (x / (1 - x)) F_2 = (x^3 / (1 - x)^3) F_0Substitute F_3 into the equation for F_0:F_0 (1 - x) = 1 + x * (x^3 / (1 - x)^3) F_0=> F_0 (1 - x) = 1 + (x^4 / (1 - x)^3) F_0Bring all F_0 terms to the left:F_0 (1 - x) - (x^4 / (1 - x)^3) F_0 = 1Factor F_0:F_0 [ (1 - x) - (x^4)/(1 - x)^3 ] = 1Compute the coefficient inside the brackets:Let me compute (1 - x) - x^4/(1 - x)^3= [ (1 - x)^4 - x^4 ] / (1 - x)^3Expand (1 - x)^4:= 1 - 4x + 6x^2 - 4x^3 + x^4Subtract x^4:= 1 - 4x + 6x^2 - 4x^3 + x^4 - x^4 = 1 - 4x + 6x^2 - 4x^3Therefore, the coefficient is (1 - 4x + 6x^2 - 4x^3)/(1 - x)^3Thus,F_0 = (1 - x)^3 / (1 - 4x + 6x^2 - 4x^3)Hmm, this seems a bit complicated. Let me see if the denominator factors. Let's try to factor 1 - 4x + 6x^2 - 4x^3.Notice that 1 - 4x + 6x^2 - 4x^3 + x^4 = (1 - x)^4, but our denominator is missing the x^4 term. So denominator is (1 - x)^4 - x^4. Wait, actually, no. Wait, 1 - 4x + 6x^2 - 4x^3 = (1 - x)^4 - x^4?Wait, let's compute (1 - x)^4:(1 - x)^4 = 1 - 4x + 6x^2 - 4x^3 + x^4So subtract x^4:(1 - x)^4 - x^4 = 1 - 4x + 6x^2 - 4x^3Therefore, denominator is (1 - x)^4 - x^4 = 1 - 4x + 6x^2 - 4x^3Therefore, F_0 = (1 - x)^3 / [ (1 - x)^4 - x^4 ] = 1 / [ (1 - x) - x^4/(1 - x)^3 ]Wait, maybe this isn't helpful. Alternatively, note that the denominator is (1 - x)^4 - x^4. Let's factor that.(1 - x)^4 - x^4 can be factored as a difference of squares:[(1 - x)^2]^2 - (x^2)^2 = [ (1 - x)^2 - x^2 ][ (1 - x)^2 + x^2 ]Compute each factor:First factor: (1 - x)^2 - x^2 = 1 - 2x + x^2 - x^2 = 1 - 2xSecond factor: (1 - x)^2 + x^2 = 1 - 2x + x^2 + x^2 = 1 - 2x + 2x^2Thus,(1 - x)^4 - x^4 = (1 - 2x)(1 - 2x + 2x^2)Therefore, F_0 = (1 - x)^3 / [ (1 - 2x)(1 - 2x + 2x^2) ]Hmm, not sure if that helps. Maybe perform partial fractions? Alternatively, note that the generating function for f(n, 3) is F_3(x) = x^3 / (1 - x)^3 * F_0(x). But given the complexity here, perhaps returning to the initial approach with generating functions and roots of unity might be more straightforward.Wait, earlier we had:Number of sequences = (1/4)[2^n + i*( (1 + i)^n - (1 - i)^n ) ]But then simplified that to:(1/4)[2^n - 2^{(n/2) + 1} sin(n /4 ) ]But maybe there's a better way to express this. Let's think about (1 + i)^n and (1 - i)^n. Let's compute these in terms of trigonometric functions.As we saw, (1 + i) is 2 (cos /4 + i sin /4), so (1 + i)^n = (2)^n [ cos(n/4) + i sin(n/4) ]Similarly, (1 - i)^n = (2)^n [ cos(n/4) - i sin(n/4) ]Therefore, (1 + i)^n - (1 - i)^n = (2)^n [ 2i sin(n/4) ]Thus, multiplying by i:i*( (1 + i)^n - (1 - i)^n ) = i*(2)^n * 2i sin(n/4) = -2*(2)^n sin(n/4 )Therefore, the number of sequences is:(1/4)[2^n - 2*(2)^n sin(n/4) ]Which can be written as:(1/4)2^n - (1/2)(2^{n/2}) sin(n/4 )But 2^{n/2} = (2)^n. Hmm.Alternatively, factor 2^n /4:Number of sequences = (2^{n-2}) - (2^{(n/2) - 1}) sin(n/4 )But this is still a bit messy. Maybe we can write this using complex numbers or express sin(n/4) in terms of exact values for different n modulo 8?Because sin(n/4) has period 8. Let me tabulate sin(n/4) for n from 0 to 7:n mod 8 | sin(n/4)0 | 01 | 2/22 | 13 | 2/24 | 05 | -2/26 | -17 | -2/2So, depending on n mod 8, sin(n/4) takes one of these values. Thus, we can express the number of sequences as:(2^{n} - 2^{(n/2)+1} sin(n/4 )) / 4But since sin(n/4) can be expressed based on n mod 8, maybe we can write different expressions for n mod 8.But this might lead to a piecewise function, which is acceptable, but maybe there's a closed-form expression.Alternatively, note that the number of sequences where the sum r mod 4 is equal to (2^n + c(n))/4, where c(n) is a term that depends on n and r. For r=3, in our case, c(n) is the imaginary part related term.But let me recall that in the generating function approach, when using roots of unity, the number of sequences is (1/4)[ (1 + 1)^n + i^{-3}(1 + i)^n + (-1)^{-3}(1 -1)^n + (-i)^{-3}(1 - i)^n ]Wait, but let's check that again. The general formula is:For each residue r mod 4, the number is (1/4) _{k=0}^3 ^{-kr}(1 + ^k)^n, where = e^{2i/4} = i.So for r=3:Number = (1/4)[ (1 + 1)^n * ^{0} + (1 + )^n * ^{-3*1} + (1 + ^2)^n * ^{-3*2} + (1 + ^3)^n * ^{-3*3} ]Simplify:= (1/4)[2^n + (1 + i)^n * ^{-3} + (1 -1)^n * ^{-6} + (1 - i)^n * ^{-9} ]As before, ^{-3} = i, ^{-6} = (-1)^3 = -1, ^{-9} = ^{-1} = -i.But (1 -1)^n = 0, so that term drops out. Thus:Number = (1/4)[2^n + (1 + i)^n * i + 0 + (1 - i)^n * (-i) ]= (1/4)[2^n + i(1 + i)^n - i(1 - i)^n ]But as we saw earlier, i(1 + i)^n - i(1 - i)^n = -2*(2)^n sin(n/4 + /2)Wait, maybe another approach. Let's compute i*(1 + i)^n.(1 + i) = 2 e^{i/4}, so (1 + i)^n = (2)^n e^{i n /4}Multiply by i: i*(1 + i)^n = (2)^n e^{i(n /4 + /2)}Similarly, -i*(1 - i)^n = -i*(2)^n e^{-i n /4} = (2)^n e^{-i(n /4 - /2)}Wait, maybe adding these terms:i*(1 + i)^n - i*(1 - i)^n = (2)^n [ e^{i(n /4 + /2)} - e^{-i(n /4 - /2)} ]Hmm, this might not be helpful. Alternatively, express in terms of sine and cosine.Alternatively, compute (1 + i)^n:(1 + i)^n = _{k=0}^n C(n,k) i^kSimilarly, (1 - i)^n = _{k=0}^n C(n,k) (-i)^kThen, i*(1 + i)^n - i*(1 - i)^n = i _{k=0}^n C(n,k) i^k - i _{k=0}^n C(n,k) (-i)^k= i _{k=0}^n C(n,k) [i^k - (-i)^k]Note that [i^k - (-i)^k] = i^k - (-1)^k i^k = i^k (1 - (-1)^k )Therefore, the sum becomes i _{k=0}^n C(n,k) i^k (1 - (-1)^k )The term (1 - (-1)^k ) is 0 when k is even, and 2 when k is odd. So we can restrict the sum to odd k:= i * 2 _{k odd} C(n,k) i^kBut i^{k} when k is odd is i^{2m +1} = i*(-1)^mThus, _{k odd} C(n,k) i^k = i _{m=0}^{floor((n-1)/2)} C(n, 2m+1) (-1)^mTherefore, the entire expression is:i * 2 * i _{m} C(n, 2m+1) (-1)^m = 2 * i^2 _{m} C(n, 2m+1) (-1)^m = -2 _{m} C(n, 2m+1) (-1)^mSo, the number of sequences is:(1/4)[2^n - 2 _{m} C(n, 2m+1) (-1)^m ]Hmm, that's an interesting expression. But I'm not sure if this simplifies further. Alternatively, note that _{m} C(n, 2m+1) (-1)^m is the imaginary part of (1 + i)^n, perhaps.Wait, (1 + i)^n = _{k=0}^n C(n,k) i^k. The imaginary part of this is _{k odd} C(n,k) i^{k -1} } because i^k = i^{2m +1} = i*(-1)^m, so the imaginary part is _{m} C(n, 2m+1) (-1)^m. Therefore, _{m} C(n, 2m+1) (-1)^m = Im[(1 + i)^n] / i. Wait, but (1 + i)^n has real and imaginary parts.Wait, actually, let me compute (1 + i)^n = a + ib, then the imaginary part is b. So _{m} C(n, 2m+1) (-1)^m is equal to the imaginary part divided by i^{1} ?Wait, maybe not. Let's take n=1:(1 + i)^1 = 1 + i. The imaginary part is 1. _{m} C(1, 2m+1) (-1)^m = C(1,1)*(-1)^0 = 1*1=1. So they match.n=2:(1 + i)^2 = 2i. Imaginary part is 2. _{m} C(2, 2m+1) (-1)^m: C(2,1)*(-1)^0 + C(2,3)... but 3>2. So only C(2,1)*1 = 2. So matches.n=3:(1 + i)^3 = -2 + 2i. Imaginary part is 2. _{m} C(3, 2m+1) (-1)^m: m=0: C(3,1)*(-1)^0=3*1=3; m=1: C(3,3)*(-1)^1=1*(-1)=-1. So total 3 -1=2. Which matches the imaginary part.So indeed, _{m} C(n, 2m+1) (-1)^m = Im[(1 + i)^n]But (1 + i)^n has imaginary part equal to this sum. Therefore, the number of sequences is:(1/4)[2^n - 2 Im( (1 + i)^n ) ]But Im( (1 + i)^n ) = (2)^n sin(n/4 )Therefore, Number = (1/4)[2^n - 2*(2)^n sin(n/4 ) ]Which is the same as before.Thus, the number of sequences is (2^{n} - 2^{(n/2)+1} sin(n/4 )) / 4 = 2^{n-2} - 2^{(n/2)-1} sin(n/4 )This seems to be the simplest form unless there's a way to express this in terms of real and imaginary parts without trigonometric functions. Alternatively, depending on n mod 8, sin(n/4) can take specific values. Let's note that sin(n/4) cycles every 8 terms. So perhaps we can write the formula in terms of n mod 8.Let me tabulate the value of sin(n/4) for n mod 8:n mod 8 | sin(n/4)0 | 01 | 2/22 | 13 | 2/24 | 05 | -2/26 | -17 | -2/2So, depending on n mod 8, the sin(n/4) term is 0, 2/2, or 1. Let's substitute these into the formula:Number of sequences = 2^{n-2} - 2^{(n/2)-1} * sin(n/4 )Let's write this as:For n 0 mod 8: sin(n/4)=0 Number = 2^{n-2}For n 1 mod 8: sin(n/4)=2/2 Number = 2^{n-2} - 2^{(n/2)-1}*(2/2) = 2^{n-2} - 2^{(n-2)/2}Similarly:For n 2 mod 8: sin(n/4)=1 Number = 2^{n-2} - 2^{(n/2)-1}*1 = 2^{n-2} - 2^{(n/2)-1}For n 3 mod 8: sin(n/4)=2/2 Same as n1: Number = 2^{n-2} - 2^{(n-2)/2}For n 4 mod 8: sin(n/4)=0 Number = 2^{n-2}For n 5 mod 8: sin(n/4)=-2/2 Number = 2^{n-2} - 2^{(n/2)-1}*(-2/2) = 2^{n-2} + 2^{(n-2)/2}For n 6 mod 8: sin(n/4)=-1 Number = 2^{n-2} - 2^{(n/2)-1}*(-1) = 2^{n-2} + 2^{(n/2)-1}For n 7 mod 8: sin(n/4)=-2/2 Same as n5: Number = 2^{n-2} + 2^{(n-2)/2}But note that in the cases where sin(n/4) is negative, subtracting a negative becomes adding. Therefore, the formula alternates between subtracting and adding terms based on n mod 8.However, this leads to a piecewise function. But maybe there's a way to express this without piecewise conditions by using (-1)^{floor(n/4)} or something similar. Alternatively, note that the expression 2^{n-2} 2^{(n/2)-1} or 2^{n-2} 2^{(n-2)/2} can be simplified.Wait, let's check for specific n:Take n=1:Number of sequences where sum 3 mod4. But sum can only be 0 or 1 for n=1. So there are 0 sequences. Let's compute using formula:2^{1-2} - 2^{(1/2)-1} * sin(/4 ) = 1/2 - 2^{-1/2}*(2/2) = 1/2 - ( (2)/2 )*(1/2) ) = 1/2 - 1/2 = 0. Hmm, let's break this down step by step.First, the sequence is of length n, and each element is either 0 or 1. So the total number of possible sequences is 2^n. That's straightforward. But we need to count only those sequences where the sum of the elements, let's call that S, satisfies S 3 mod 4. So essentially, S mod 4 should equal 3. I remember that when dealing with such problems, generating functions can be a useful tool. Generating functions can encode the number of ways to achieve certain sums, especially in combinatorics. Let me recall how that works.For each element in the sequence, since it can be 0 or 1, the generating function for a single element is 1 + x. Here, the term 1 represents choosing 0 (which contributes nothing to the sum), and x represents choosing 1 (which contributes 1 to the sum). Since there are n elements, the generating function for the entire sequence would be (1 + x)^n. Expanding this generating function gives us the coefficients of x^k, which represent the number of sequences with exactly k 1s. So, the coefficient of x^k in (1 + x)^n is C(n, k), the binomial coefficient. But we need the sum of coefficients where k 3 mod 4. So, essentially, we need to compute the sum of C(n, k) for all k such that k 3 mod 4.Now, how do we extract such coefficients from the generating function? There's a technique using roots of unity to pick out specific terms in a generating function based on their exponents modulo some number. Since we're dealing with modulo 4, we can use the 4th roots of unity, which are 1, i, -1, and -i.The formula to extract the sum of coefficients where k r mod m is (1/m) * _{j=0}^{m-1} ^{-jr} * f(^j), where is a primitive m-th root of unity. Therefore, this is the answer.Final Answerboxed{dfrac{2^n - 2^{frac{n}{2} + 1} sinleft(dfrac{npi}{4}right)}{4}}</think>To find the number of binary sequences of length ( n ) such that the sum of the elements leaves a remainder of 3 when divided by 4, we use generating functions and roots of unity.1. Generating Function Approach: The generating function for a sequence of 0s and 1s is ( (1 + x)^n ). We need the sum of coefficients of ( x^k ) where ( k equiv 3 mod 4 ).2. Roots of Unity Filter: Using the 4th roots of unity (1, ( i ), -1, ( -i )), we filter the coefficients: [ frac{1}{4} left[ (1 + 1)^n + i cdot (1 + i)^n - (1 - 1)^n - i cdot (1 - i)^n right] ] Simplifying, since ( (1 - 1)^n = 0 ): [ frac{1}{4} left[ 2^n + i cdot (1 + i)^n - i cdot (1 - i)^n right] ]3. Polar Form Conversion: Convert ( (1 + i)^n ) and ( (1 - i)^n ) to polar form: [ (1 + i)^n = 2^{n/2} left( cos frac{npi}{4} + i sin frac{npi}{4} right) ] [ (1 - i)^n = 2^{n/2} left( cos frac{npi}{4} - i sin frac{npi}{4} right) ]4. Simplifying the Expression: Substitute these into the generating function expression: [ i cdot (1 + i)^n - i cdot (1 - i)^n = 2^{n/2} left( -2 sin frac{npi}{4} right) ] Therefore, the number of sequences is: [ frac{1}{4} left[ 2^n - 2^{n/2 + 1} sin frac{npi}{4} right] ]5. Final Formula: The number of sequences is given by: [ boxed{dfrac{2^n - 2^{frac{n}{2} + 1} sinleft(dfrac{npi}{4}right)}{4}} ]This formula accounts for the periodic nature of the sine function and correctly computes the number of binary sequences with the required sum modulo 4.]

:To solve the equation (x^2 + y^2 - 5xy + 5 = 0) for positive integers (x) and (y), we can proceed as follows:1. Rewrite the equation: [ x^2 + y^2 - 5xy + 5 = 0 ]2. Consider the equation as a quadratic in (x): [ x^2 - 5xy + y^2 + 5 = 0 ]3. Solve for (x) using the quadratic formula: The quadratic formula (ax^2 + bx + c = 0) gives the roots as: [ x = frac{-b pm sqrt{b^2 - 4ac}}{2a} ] Here, (a = 1), (b = -5y), and (c = y^2 + 5). Substituting these values, we get: [ x = frac{5y pm sqrt{(5y)^2 - 4(y^2 + 5)}}{2} ] Simplify the expression under the square root: [ x = frac{5y pm sqrt{25y^2 - 4y^2 - 20}}{2} ] [ x = frac{5y pm sqrt{21y^2 - 20}}{2} ]4. Ensure the discriminant is a perfect square: For (x) to be an integer, (sqrt{21y^2 - 20}) must be an integer. Let (k = sqrt{21y^2 - 20}), then: [ 21y^2 - 20 = k^2 ] Rearrange to form a Diophantine equation: [ 21y^2 - k^2 = 20 ] This is a Pell-like equation. We need to find integer solutions ((y, k)).5. Check for small values of (y): - For (y = 1): [ 21(1)^2 - k^2 = 20 implies 21 - k^2 = 20 implies k^2 = 1 implies k = 1 ] Substituting (y = 1) and (k = 1) back into the quadratic formula: [ x = frac{5(1) pm 1}{2} = frac{5 pm 1}{2} ] [ x = 3 text{ or } 2 ] Thus, ((x, y) = (3, 1)) or ((2, 1)). - For (y = 2): [ 21(2)^2 - k^2 = 20 implies 84 - k^2 = 20 implies k^2 = 64 implies k = 8 ] Substituting (y = 2) and (k = 8) back into the quadratic formula: [ x = frac{5(2) pm 8}{2} = frac{10 pm 8}{2} ] [ x = 9 text{ or } 1 ] Thus, ((x, y) = (9, 2)) or ((1, 2)).6. Verify the solutions: - For ((x, y) = (3, 1)): [ 3^2 + 1^2 - 5 cdot 3 cdot 1 + 5 = 9 + 1 - 15 + 5 = 0 ] - For ((x, y) = (2, 1)): [ 2^2 + 1^2 - 5 cdot 2 cdot 1 + 5 = 4 + 1 - 10 + 5 = 0 ] - For ((x, y) = (9, 2)): [ 9^2 + 2^2 - 5 cdot 9 cdot 2 + 5 = 81 + 4 - 90 + 5 = 0 ] - For ((x, y) = (1, 2)): [ 1^2 + 2^2 - 5 cdot 1 cdot 2 + 5 = 1 + 4 - 10 + 5 = 0 ]Thus, the pairs of positive integers ((x, y)) that satisfy the equation are ((3, 1)), ((2, 1)), ((9, 2)), and ((1, 2)).The final answer is ( boxed{ (3, 1) } ), ((2, 1)), ((9, 2)), and ((1, 2)).

:Solution. The car base will work normally (event D) if eight (event A), nine (event B), or all ten (event C) cars go out on the line. According to the theorem of the addition of probabilities,P(D)=P(A)+P(B)+P(C)=P_{10}(8)+P_{10}(9)+P_{10}(10)Each term will be found using the Bernoulli formula.Since the probability of each car not going out on the line is 0.1, the probability of a car going out on the line will be 0.9, i.e., p=0.9, q=0.1. From the condition, it follows that n=10, k=8,9,10. Therefore,begin{gathered}P(D)=C_{10}^{8} cdot(0.9)^{8} cdot(0.1)^{2}+C_{10}^{9} cdot(0.9)^{9} cdot 0.1+C_{10}^{10} cdot(0.9)^{10} cdot(0.1)^{0} approx approx 0.1937+0.3874+0.3487=0.9298end{gathered}

:Consider the given quadratic equation in terms of x:9x - (4 + a) cdot 3x + 4 = 0Let's rewrite the equation as a standard quadratic form:(3x)^2 - (4 + a) cdot 3x + 4 = 0By Vieta's formulas, for a quadratic equation ax^2 + bx + c = 0, the product of the roots (x_1 cdot x_2) is equal to c / a and the sum of the roots (x_1 + x_2) is equal to -b / a. So we apply this to our equation:3x_1 cdot 3x_2 = 4 implies x_1 cdot x_2 = frac{4}{9}3x_1 + 3x_2 = 4 + a implies x_1 + x_2 = frac{4 + a}{3}We know that the sum of x_1 and x_2 is frac{4 + a}{3}, so let's find another expression for x_1 + x_2:x_1 + x_2 = 2 cdot log_3 2By inequality of arithmetic and geometric means (AM-GM inequality), we have:frac{x_1 + x_2}{2} geq sqrt{x_1 cdot x_2}Replacing x_1 + x_2 and x_1 cdot x_2 with the corresponding expressions, we get:frac{2 cdot log_3 2}{2} geq sqrt{frac{4}{9}}(log_3 2)^2 geq frac{4}{9}Squaring both sides of x_1 + x_2 = 2 cdot log_3 2, we find:(x_1 + x_2)^2 = 4 cdot (log_3 2)^2The expression for a can now be written as follows using the sum and product of roots:a = 3 cdot (x_1 + x_2) - 4a = 3 cdot 2 cdot log_3 2 - 4a = 6 cdot log_3 2 - 4Since (log_3 2)^2 geq frac{4}{9}, the minimum value of (log_3 2)^2 is frac{4}{9}. Consequently, the minimum value of a is achieved when (log_3 2)^2 is at its minimum:a_{text{min}} = 6 cdot sqrt{frac{4}{9}} - 4a_{text{min}} = 6 cdot frac{2}{3} - 4a_{text{min}} = 4 - 4a_{text{min}} = 0Hence, the minimum value of a is boxed{0}.

:1. Reflecting the Orthocenter: - It is well known that reflecting the orthocenter ( H ) of a triangle over any side of the triangle results in a point that lies on the circumcircle of the triangle. This is a consequence of the properties of the nine-point circle and the reflection properties of the orthocenter.2. Defining Points: - Let ( P ) and ( Q ) be the reflections of ( H ) over ( BA ) and ( BC ) respectively. By the reflection property, ( P ) and ( Q ) lie on the circumcircle of ( triangle ABC ).3. Equal Distances: - Since ( P ) and ( Q ) are reflections of ( H ), we have: [ HP = HQ = BP = BQ = R ] where ( R ) is the circumradius of ( triangle ABC ).4. Collinearity: - The points ( P, A_0, C_0, Q ) are collinear because ( A_0 ) and ( C_0 ) are the midpoints of ( BP ) and ( BQ ) respectively, and ( P ) and ( Q ) are reflections of ( H ).5. Rhombus Properties: - The quadrilaterals ( BOAP ) and ( BOCQ ) are rhombi because ( P ) and ( Q ) are reflections of ( H ) and thus equidistant from ( B ). This implies: [ AP parallel OB parallel CQ ]6. Parallelogram: - Since ( AP parallel OB parallel CQ ), the quadrilateral ( ACPQ ) forms a parallelogram. Therefore, the opposite sides are equal: [ PQ = AC ]7. Perimeter Calculation: - From the properties of the rhombus and the parallelogram, we know: [ PA_0 = A_0O quad text{and} quad QC_0 = C_0O ] - Therefore, the perimeter of ( triangle A_0OC_0 ) is: [ A_0O + OC_0 + A_0C_0 = PA_0 + QC_0 = AC ]Thus, the perimeter of ( triangle A_0OC_0 ) is equal to ( AC ).(blacksquare)The final answer is ( boxed{ AC } )
